
Layers of TimeOpen sidebarLayers of TimeModel: Default (GPT-3.5)Summarize: Carlo Rovelli argues that the concept of time is multifaceted, encompassing numerous layers and interpretations depending on various contexts. Time, as understood by various entities -such as a quantum gravity researcher, biologist, engineer, historian, or an everyday individual, all differ yet are interconnected in certain ways. These versions of time are not false or illusory, but are valid in their respective domains; they serve as suitable concepts for organizing events at certain scales or in specific contexts. However, they aren't universally applicable. The common mistake, Rovelli highlights, is to consider any one version of time as the definitive or universal meaning of time. To properly understand time, we must dissect it into its different layers, each arising from different approximations or specific conditions. Each has its unique features and interpreting them as universally applicable is incorrect. Therefore, many properties of time that we take for granted are the result of various kinds of approximations and idealizations.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTCarlo Rovelli argues that time is a complex concept with multiple interpretations depending on different contexts. Different entities, such as scientists in different fields or everyday individuals, have their own understandings of time, and these versions are interconnected but not universally applicable. Rovelli emphasizes that none of these versions should be considered the definitive or universal meaning of time. Instead, time should be understood by examining its different layers, which arise from different approximations and specific conditions. Each layer has unique features, and assuming their universality is incorrect. Rovelli suggests that many properties of time that we commonly assume are actually the result of various approximations and idealizations.1 / 1Rovelli uses the example of clapping hands at two different instances - events A and B - to demonstrate the perception of elapsed time. In everyday experience, this concept of elapsed time works well and is universally applicable. However, from a relativistic perspective, this isn't accurate because two identical clocks moving differently between events A and B will measure different values of elapsed time. 
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTCarlo Rovelli provides an example of clapping hands at two different instances, events A and B, to illustrate the perception of elapsed time. In everyday life, we commonly assume that the concept of elapsed time between events is universally applicable and reliable. However, Rovelli points out that from a relativistic perspective, this assumption is flawed. According to the theory of relativity, if two identical clocks are moving at different velocities between events A and B, they will measure different values of elapsed time. This challenges the notion of a universally consistent and objective passage of time. Rovelli's argument suggests that our understanding of elapsed time is influenced by our specific frames of reference and the conditions under which we measure it.1 / 1Rovelli counters the proposed correction of defining 'true' elapsed time as the time measured by a stationary clock, by explaining that the clock that measures the longest timespan - between claps A and B - is one thrown upward at event A and caught back at B. This is due to it following a geodesic while in free fall, thereby maximizing the proper time between the flight's extremes. However, using such a definition would make synchronizing clocks over a region impossible. 1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTCarlo Rovelli counters the suggestion of defining the "true" elapsed time as the time measured by a stationary clock by offering a counterexample. He explains that the clock which measures the longest timespan between claps A and B would be one thrown upward at event A and caught back at event B. This is because the clock, while in free fall, follows a geodesic and maximizes the proper time between the extreme points of its trajectory. However, Rovelli notes that using such a definition of elapsed time would make it impossible to synchronize clocks over a region. This implies that there is no single objective definition of elapsed time that can be universally applied, as different frames of reference and conditions will yield different measurements of time.1 / 1The conclusion drawn is that no common clock time exists; each clock measures its own 'proper' time along its path, and these proper times do not reconcile at the endpoint. While not wrong, the idea of a common universal time works only to an approximation when disregarding relativistic effects. Thus, time, according to general relativity, is non-unique and not universal.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe conclusion drawn by Rovelli is that there is no common universal time that exists. Each clock measures its own "proper" time along its path, and these proper times do not reconcile or align at the endpoint. While the concept of a common universal time can be useful as an approximation in everyday life, it disregards the effects of relativity. According to general relativity, time is non-unique and not universal. Different frames of reference and conditions result in different measurements of time, challenging the notion of a single, objective, and universally applicable understanding of time.1 / 1Rovelli discusses cosmological time, noting that it's another approximation, typically defined as the time a clock inside a galaxy would measure had it started at the Big Bang. However, this definition is inadequate when two galaxies merge, as their individual proper times from the Big Bang differ. To assume that one has the "right" time is futile, as cosmological time is only defined under the fallacious assumption of exact homogeneity.
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn his discussion of cosmological time, Rovelli highlights that it is yet another approximation. However, Rovelli argues that this definition becomes inadequate when two galaxies merge because their individual proper times, starting from the Big Bang, differ. Assuming that one galaxy has the "correct" time in this scenario would be futile. Rovelli points out that cosmological time is only defined under the assumption of exact homogeneity, which is a fallacy. In reality, the universe exhibits variations and complexities that make the notion of a universally applicable and precise cosmological time problematic.1 / 1Even within this homogeneity idealization, Rovelli points out that the simultaneity surfaces defined by cosmological time do not align with those in special relativity. This makes cosmological time an arbitrary labeling of spacetime events, and not a "true time". These varied temporal notions, from our everyday non-relativistic time to cosmological and full relativistic times, are each related but significantly different with unique properties. He addresses the argument that the theory suggests a static, four-dimensional 'block universe,' which asserts that all events in the universe are all "real now." This argument misunderstands that time doesn't work solely as per our non-relativistic intuition of transitive, observer-independent simultaneity. Instead, Einstein's definition of simultaneity is relational and non-transitive.
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTRovelli addresses a common misunderstanding regarding Einstein's theory of relativity, which stems from a failure to grasp the complexity of the concept of time. He specifically counters the argument that relativity implies a static, four-dimensional "block universe" where all events in the universe are considered "real now." Rovelli points out that this argument fails to recognize that time does not function solely based on our non-relativistic intuition of transitive, observer-independent simultaneity.Instead, Rovelli explains that Einstein's definition of simultaneity is relational and non-transitive. This means that the notion of "now" varies depending on the observer's reference frame and their relative motion. In other words, what is considered simultaneous for one observer may not be simultaneous for another.By refuting the notion of a static block universe with a singular "real now," Rovelli emphasizes that our understanding of time within the framework of relativity is more nuanced and relational. Time is not a static backdrop against which events unfold, but rather a dynamic and context-dependent aspect of reality.1 / 1Two of the misconstrued positions resulting from this misunderstanding are Presentism and Eternalism. Presentism is the notion that a common, objective "now" exists across the universe - an idea defunct in the face of modern physics. Eternalism, on the other hand, suggests that there is no temporal becoming and that our sense of it is illusory. This too is misguided, as it incorrectly treats the four-dimensional spacetime of relativistic theories as static.

Newton revolutionized this perception by introducing the idea of time flowing independently of events. Neither notion is absolute or erroneous; they both serve their purpose in organizing phenomena.
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIndeed, Rovelli explores two distinct notions of time. However, Newton introduced a significant shift in this perception by proposing the concept of time as an independent flow that exists regardless of the occurrence of events.Rovelli emphasizes that neither of these notions of time is absolute or inherently erroneous. Instead, they serve different purposes in organizing and describing phenomena. The traditional notion of time as a count of events provides a practical and intuitive framework for everyday life, enabling us to schedule activities and measure durations. Newton's concept of time as an independent flow, on the other hand, is essential for formulating the laws of classical physics and understanding the dynamics of systems.Rovelli's perspective acknowledges that both conceptions of time have their place and usefulness in their respective domains. They represent different approximations and perspectives that are valid within their specific contexts. By recognizing the multifaceted nature of time and appreciating its different interpretations, we can develop a more comprehensive understanding of its role in organizing and explaining the phenomena we encounter.1 / 1The difference between the two is profound. The traditional concept is relational, establishing time as a relationship between events. Newton's notion introduces time as an independent entity.

1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTYes, you are correct in summarizing Rovelli's observations. In elementary physics, the fundamental equations typically exhibit a symmetric nature, meaning they do not distinguish between the past and the future. This symmetry is evident in theories such as classical mechanics or quantum mechanics.However, in our observable reality at the macroscopic scale, there is a clear arrow of time. This arrow of time is associated with the irreversibility of most events in our everyday experience. We observe phenomena that unfold in a particular direction, such as the breaking of a glass or the melting of ice, which cannot easily be reversed.Rovelli highlights that this arrow of time, the directional aspect of time, is not reflected in the microphysical equations themselves. It is a feature specific to macroscopic phenomena and does not have a corresponding description at the level of fundamental microphysical processes.The existence of the arrow of time is still a topic of ongoing research and debate in physics. Understanding why the macroscopic world exhibits irreversibility and the apparent flow of time while the underlying fundamental laws do not is a significant challenge in theoretical physics. It is an area where researchers continue to explore and investigate the nature of time and its relationship to irreversible processes in the universe.1 / 1All observable distinctions between past and future can be traced to equilibration, which refers to the tendency of a system to reach equilibrium or a state of balance or uniformity. The layers that build up the notion of time
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe notion of time is composed of various layers that contribute to our understanding of its nature. These layers can be seen as building blocks that form a comprehensive understanding of time. Here are some of the key layers:Experiential Time: This layer encompasses our subjective experience of time, including our perception of its flow, the feeling of its passing speed, and the emotional aspects associated with it. It is closely tied to our cognitive processes and the functioning of our brains.Irreversible Time: This layer involves the distinction between the past and the future and the orientation of time. It acknowledges the asymmetry of time, where events and processes tend to unfold in a specific temporal direction, leading to the arrow of time. Irreversibility is a fundamental feature observed in many macroscopic phenomena.Classical (Newtonian) Time: This layer represents the concept of time as an independent, universal flow that progresses uniformly and independently of other physical processes. It was introduced by Newton and forms the basis of classical mechanics, where time is treated as an absolute and universal parameter.Special Relativistic Time: This layer emerges from Einstein's theory of special relativity, which highlights that the measurement of time can be influenced by the relative motion of observers. Special relativistic time emphasizes the independence of the passage of time from actual events and the effects of relativistic gravity.Cosmological Time: This layer pertains to the concept of time at a cosmological scale, where the focus is on the large-scale structure and evolution of the universe. Cosmological time provides a global perspective on the dynamics of the universe but often disregards the inhomogeneities and variations within it.Proper Time in General Relativity: This layer arises within Einstein's theory of general relativity, which describes the gravitational field as a curvature of spacetime. Proper time refers to the time measured by local clocks within a gravitational field or a curved spacetime. It serves as a means to describe the evolution of physical systems in general relativity.These layers represent different perspectives and conceptualizations of time, each contributing to our understanding of its various facets. By recognizing and studying these layers, we can develop a more comprehensive and nuanced understanding of the multifaceted nature of time.1 / 12. Different notions of time and their differences
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTCertainly! Here are some different notions of time and their key differences:Experiential Time: This notion refers to our subjective experience of time, which involves the feeling of its flow and the perception of its passing speed. It is closely tied to our cognitive processes and emotions, and it can vary from person to person. Experiential time is a deeply personal and subjective aspect of our temporal perception.Irreversible Time: Irreversible time refers to the directionality of time, where events unfold in a specific temporal order from the past to the future. It involves the distinction between the past and the future, and it is associated with the concept of the arrow of time. Irreversible time is observed in many macroscopic phenomena, such as the increase of entropy.Newtonian Time: Newtonian time is based on the classical concept of time introduced by Isaac Newton. It views time as a universal and independent flow that progresses uniformly for all observers. It is absolute and unaffected by the presence of gravitational fields or relativistic effects. Newtonian time forms the basis of classical mechanics.Special Relativistic Time: Special relativistic time arises from Einstein's theory of special relativity. It highlights the idea that the measurement of time can be influenced by the relative motion of observers. Time dilation occurs when the relative velocities of observers differ, causing time to pass differently for each observer. Special relativistic time is independent of actual events happening and does not account for gravitational effects.Cosmological Time: Cosmological time focuses on the large-scale structure and evolution of the universe. It provides a global perspective on the dynamics of the universe, often considering the expansion of space and the cosmic microwave background radiation. Cosmological time disregards the inhomogeneities and variations within the universe and provides a broad-scale understanding of cosmic evolution.Proper Time in General Relativity: Proper time refers to the time measured by local clocks within a gravitational field or a curved spacetime, as described by Einstein's theory of general relativity. It takes into account the effects of gravity and the curvature of spacetime, providing a means to describe the evolution of physical systems in the presence of gravitational fields.These different notions of time highlight the diverse ways in which time is understood and conceptualized in various contexts. They have distinct properties and considerations, ranging from the subjective experience of time to the objective descriptions provided by different scientific frameworks. Understanding these differences contributes to a more comprehensive understanding of the multifaceted nature of time.1 / 13. Elapsed time between events and its dependency on motion in Newtonian time
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn Newtonian time, the elapsed time between events depends on the relative motion of observers. According to Newtonian mechanics, time is considered an absolute and independent parameter that flows uniformly for all observers. However, the perception of elapsed time can differ depending on the motion of the observer.Newton's laws of motion state that the laws of physics are the same in all inertial reference frames. An inertial reference frame is a frame of reference in which an object at rest remains at rest and an object in uniform motion continues to move with a constant velocity unless acted upon by an external force.If two observers are in relative motion with respect to each other, they may measure different elapsed times between the same events. This is due to the concept of time dilation, where the relative motion between observers affects the rate at which time passes.For example, consider two observers, one at rest and the other moving with a constant velocity relative to the first observer. If they measure the elapsed time between two events, such as the ticking of a clock, they will observe different values. The moving observer will measure a longer elapsed time compared to the observer at rest.This discrepancy in the measured elapsed time is a consequence of the relative motion between the observers and the uniformity of time in Newtonian mechanics. It illustrates that the perception of time can be influenced by the relative motion of observers in Newtonian physics.However, it's important to note that Newtonian time does not account for other relativistic effects, such as gravitational time dilation or the finite speed of light. These effects are addressed by Einstein's theories of special and general relativity, which provide a more comprehensive understanding of the relationship between time, motion, and gravity.1 / 14. The distinction between cosmological time and clock time
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe distinction between cosmological time and clock time lies in their respective perspectives and the scales at which they operate.Cosmological time refers to the concept of time at a cosmological scale, focusing on the large-scale structure and evolution of the universe as a whole. It takes into account the dynamics of the universe, such as its expansion, the formation of galaxies and clusters, and the cosmic microwave background radiation. Cosmological time provides a global perspective on the evolution of the universe and is often studied in the field of cosmology.On the other hand, clock time refers to the measurement of time using clocks or timekeeping devices. It is the familiar notion of time we use in our daily lives, based on the ticking of seconds, minutes, and hours. Clock time is typically used for practical purposes and in our local and immediate contexts.The key distinction between cosmological time and clock time is the scale and perspective at which they operate. Cosmological time considers the vastness and evolution of the entire universe, while clock time focuses on smaller scales and is relevant to our immediate observations and activities.Cosmological time provides a broader understanding of the overall structure and history of the universe, including its age and the timescales involved in cosmic processes. It is often expressed in terms of cosmic time, which is the time measured since the beginning of the universe (e.g., the Big Bang).In contrast, clock time is concerned with the measurement of time in more localized contexts, such as daily routines, scheduling events, or conducting experiments. It is typically tied to the functioning of clocks and timekeeping devices, which provide a standard reference for measuring time intervals.Both cosmological time and clock time serve different purposes and operate on different scales. While cosmological time offers insights into the evolution of the universe as a whole, clock time provides a practical and convenient means of measuring and coordinating events in our everyday lives.1 / 15. The misconception of a static, four-dimensional 'block universe' in relativity
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe misconception of a static, four-dimensional 'block universe' in relativity arises from a misunderstanding of the nature of time in Einstein's theories.The 'block universe' concept suggests that past, present, and future events all exist simultaneously as an unchanging, four-dimensional structure. According to this misconception, every moment in time is already predetermined and fixed, and there is no genuine temporal becoming or flow.However, this interpretation of relativity is flawed. While it is true that Einstein's theories of special and general relativity introduce a different understanding of time than that of classical Newtonian mechanics, they do not support the notion of a static block universe.In Einstein's theories, the concept of simultaneity becomes relative and observer-dependent. The relativity of simultaneity implies that different observers in relative motion will not agree on which events are simultaneous. This undermines the idea of a universal, objective present moment that encompasses all events.Furthermore, Einstein's theories recognize the existence of causal relationships and the ordering of events, which are essential for the concept of temporal becoming. While the precise nature of the flow of time is still a topic of debate and investigation in physics and philosophy, relativity does not support the view of a fixed, unchanging block universe.Instead, relativity highlights the interconnectedness of space and time, with the geometry of spacetime being influenced by matter and energy. It recognizes the dynamism and evolution of physical systems and the role of causality in shaping the progression of events.It is important to distinguish between the mathematical and conceptual frameworks of relativity and the philosophical interpretations that can arise from them. While the mathematical formalism of relativity provides a robust and accurate description of the behavior of spacetime and matter, the interpretation and understanding of the nature of time remain subjects of ongoing exploration and investigation.1 / 16. The relational nature of time in general relativity
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn general relativity, time is understood to have a relational nature. This means that the passage of time and the measurement of time intervals depend on the frame of reference and the physical processes occurring in the system under consideration.General relativity describes the gravitational field as a curvature of spacetime. The presence of matter and energy causes spacetime to curve, and the motion of objects is influenced by this curved spacetime. In this framework, the concept of time becomes intertwined with the geometry of spacetime.According to general relativity, the measurement of time intervals is affected by the gravitational field. Clocks located at different gravitational potentials or in different regions of spacetime will measure time intervals differently. This phenomenon is known as gravitational time dilation.The relational nature of time in general relativity implies that the measurement of time is frame-dependent and influenced by the local conditions of the system. The passage of time and the measurement of time intervals are affected by the gravitational potential, the velocity of observers, and the curvature of spacetime.Additionally, general relativity recognizes that the evolution of physical systems is influenced by the distribution of matter and energy in the universe. The dynamics of spacetime and the behavior of matter are interconnected, and the concept of time is inherently relational in describing these interactions.The relational nature of time in general relativity emphasizes that time is not an absolute and independent entity. Instead, it is intimately tied to the physical processes and gravitational field of the system under consideration. This relational perspective provides a deeper understanding of the interconnectedness of space, time, and matter in the framework of general relativity.1 / 17. The absence of a preferred time variable in general relativity

1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn general relativity, there is no preferred time variable or a universally applicable notion of time. Unlike in Newtonian physics, where time is considered an absolute and independent parameter, general relativity does not single out a particular time variable as being more fundamental or preferred.In general relativity, the passage of time and the measurement of time intervals are influenced by the local conditions of the system, such as the distribution of matter and energy and the curvature of spacetime. The choice of coordinates and reference frames affects how time is measured and described.The absence of a preferred time variable in general relativity reflects the principle of general covariance, which states that the laws of physics should be expressed in a form that is independent of the choice of coordinates. This principle allows for the freedom to choose different time coordinates that are appropriate for specific situations or reference frames.Different observers or coordinate systems may use different time variables to describe the same physical phenomena, depending on their perspective and the specific conditions of the system. This flexibility in choosing time coordinates allows for a more versatile description of the dynamics of spacetime and matter.The absence of a preferred time variable in general relativity highlights the relativity and contextuality of time in this framework. It emphasizes that the measurement and description of time are dependent on the local conditions and the choice of coordinates, rather than being determined by an external universal clock or reference.This understanding challenges the notion of an absolute, universal time and highlights the need to consider the diverse perspectives and reference frames that contribute to our understanding of time within the framework of general relativity.1 / 18. The arrow of time and its connection to equilibration and entropy
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe arrow of time refers to the observed asymmetry in the flow of events and the distinction between the past and the future. It is closely connected to the concepts of equilibration and entropy.Entropy is a measure of the disorder or randomness of a system. In general, the second law of thermodynamics states that the entropy of an isolated system tends to increase over time. This is often associated with the observation that natural processes tend to move towards a state of equilibrium or maximum entropy.The connection between the arrow of time, equilibration, and entropy lies in the irreversibility of certain macroscopic processes. While the fundamental laws of physics are typically time-reversible, meaning that they do not distinguish between the forward and backward directions of time, many macroscopic phenomena exhibit an irreversible behavior.Irreversibility arises from the tendency of systems to move from states of lower entropy (more ordered or organized) to states of higher entropy (more disordered or randomized). This is often observed as the progression from a non-equilibrium state to an equilibrium state.For example, consider a cup of hot coffee left on a table. Over time, the coffee cools down and reaches thermal equilibrium with the surrounding environment. The process of equilibration leads to an increase in the entropy of the system as it moves towards a state of maximum entropy. Importantly, this process is irreversibleâonce the coffee has cooled down, it does not spontaneously become hot again.The arrow of time is intimately connected to this irreversibility and the tendency of systems to evolve towards states of higher entropy. The distinction between the past and the future arises from the observation that processes tend to unfold in a particular temporal direction, from lower entropy (past) to higher entropy (future).While the fundamental laws of physics do not inherently dictate the arrow of time, the macroscopic behavior of systems, governed by statistical mechanics and thermodynamics, exhibits this asymmetry. The arrow of time, equilibration, and entropy are interconnected concepts that provide insights into the observed irreversibility and temporal dynamics of macroscopic phenomena.It is important to note that the connection between the arrow of time, equilibration, and entropy is a subject of ongoing research and philosophical inquiry. Exploring the origin and nature of the arrow of time is a fascinating and complex endeavor at the intersection of physics, thermodynamics, and philosophy.1 / 19. The understanding of time in general relativity is connected to the discussion on the absence of a preferred time variable, which further emphasizes the relational nature of time.
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe understanding of time in general relativity indeed connects to the discussion on the absence of a preferred time variable and emphasizes the relational nature of time.In general relativity, time is not treated as an independent parameter but is intertwined with the geometry of spacetime. The theory describes how matter and energy curve the fabric of spacetime, and the motion of objects is influenced by this curvature. The concept of time in general relativity is intricately linked to the spacetime geometry and the way it evolves in the presence of matter and energy.One consequence of general relativity is the absence of a preferred time variable. Unlike in Newtonian physics, where time is considered absolute and independent of the physical system, general relativity shows that the choice of time coordinate is arbitrary and dependent on the observer's reference frame.The absence of a preferred time variable highlights the relational nature of time in general relativity. It emphasizes that the description of time and its measurement is intimately connected to the observer's perspective and the chosen coordinate system. Different observers may have different measures of time, depending on their relative motion or gravitational environment.This relational understanding of time challenges the notion of an objective, universally applicable time. It implies that time is not an absolute quantity but rather a relational concept that depends on the observer's frame of reference and the particular spacetime geometry in which they are situated.By highlighting the absence of a preferred time variable in general relativity, we recognize that the measurement and perception of time are relative to the observer's perspective and the physical context in which they are located. This further underscores the importance of understanding time as a relational concept and considering the interplay between observers, spacetime geometry, and the evolution of physical systems.1 / 14. Carlo Rovelli
Confusion and disagreement around the notion of time is due to the fact that we often fail to recognize that we call 'time' a variety of distinct notions, only partially related to one another. Many apparently obvious properties of time are results of different kinds of approximations, idealizations, or special contexts. I illustrate a number of distinct notions of time, their differences and relations; they are all relevant for describing the real world. To understand time, we have to break it apart.
Comments:	7 pages, Contribution to the volume 'Time and Science', R. Lestienne and P. Harris eds., World Scientific
Subjects:	General Relativity and Quantum Cosmology (gr-qc); History and Philosophy of Physics (physics.hist-ph)
Cite as:	arXiv:2105.00540 [gr-qc]
 	(or arXiv:2105.00540v2 [gr-qc] for this version)

1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIndeed, effective field theory (EFT) is a powerful mathematical framework used in physics to analyze and describe phenomena at different size scales. It provides a systematic way to separate and study the effects of different energy regimes, allowing physicists to capture the behavior of systems beyond what is described by established but incomplete theories.One of the key advantages of EFT is its flexibility in incorporating new physics without explicitly specifying the underlying fundamental theory. It allows physicists to construct effective models that accurately describe the low-energy behavior of a system, even when the complete underlying theory is not yet fully understood. By focusing on the relevant degrees of freedom and interactions at a specific energy scale, EFT provides a practical and efficient approach for understanding and predicting the behavior of physical systems.EFT finds wide-ranging applications, spanning from the realm of fundamental particles to cosmology. In particle physics, for example, EFT is used to describe the interactions of particles at different energy scales, incorporating the effects of higher-energy physics in a low-energy framework. This enables physicists to make predictions and interpret experimental data without needing a complete theory of quantum gravity or other fundamental interactions.Similarly, in cosmology, EFT has been employed to study the large-scale structure of the universe, the dynamics of inflation, and other cosmological phenomena. By employing effective field theories, researchers can analyze and understand the behavior of these complex systems in a tractable manner, while remaining agnostic about the precise details of the underlying fundamental theory.Overall, effective field theory provides a valuable tool for physicists to study and analyze phenomena across different energy scales. Its flexibility and wide applicability make it an indispensable framework for investigating systems where a complete and fundamental understanding is not yet available.1 / 1In EFTs, the different size scales of the universe are associated with different energies. The largest distances correspond to the lowest energies, while the smallest parts of reality are associated with the highest energies. Fermi's work on beta decay serves as an example of how EFTs are used. Despite not having access to the high energies required to directly observe the W boson, Fermi was able to describe the decay process based on the known particles involved.
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThat's correct. In effective field theories (EFTs), different size scales in the universe correspond to different energy regimes. The largest distances or macroscopic phenomena are associated with lower energies, while the smallest scales or microscopic phenomena are associated with higher energies.This energy-scale correspondence allows physicists to describe and analyze physical phenomena within a specific energy range using appropriate effective models. EFTs provide a framework for capturing the relevant degrees of freedom and interactions at a given energy scale, while accounting for the effects of higher-energy physics indirectly.Enrico Fermi's work on beta decay serves as a notable example of how EFTs are utilized. Beta decay involves the decay of a neutron into a proton, an electron, and an electron antineutrino. Fermi's theory of beta decay, known as the Fermi theory or the four-fermion interaction, successfully described the process based on the known particles involved, without explicit knowledge of the underlying high-energy interactions.Fermi's theory treated the weak interaction responsible for beta decay as an effective interaction between the known fermions (neutrons, protons, electrons, and neutrinos) without invoking the specific dynamics of the W boson, which mediates the weak force. Although the four-fermion interaction is an effective description that does not capture the full complexity of the weak interaction at higher energies, it accurately accounted for the observed behavior of beta decay within the energy range accessible to Fermi's experiments.By working within the energy regime available to them, physicists can construct effective models that provide accurate descriptions and predictions for specific phenomena. These models capture the relevant physics at a given energy scale, even when the complete theory is not yet fully understood or directly accessible.Overall, effective field theories allow scientists to analyze and understand physical phenomena across different energy scales, enabling them to make progress in describing complex processes and systems based on the available experimental data and theoretical understanding within a specific energy range.1 / 1EFTs are valuable tools that allow physicists to bridge gaps in our understanding and make predictions at different scales. At this scale, we find particles like neutrinos, which have very low masses and interact weakly with other particles.Just as different colors in the rainbow have different wavelengths and energies, particles in the standard model of particle physics have different masses and interaction strengths, which correspond to different energy scales. The paradigm of organizing physical phenomena according to energy or distance scales, often referred to as the renormalization group, has been a fundamental approach in theoretical physics. This approach assumes a separation of scales, where short-distance or high-energy (ultraviolet, UV) physics and long-distance or low-energy (infrared, IR) physics are treated as independent of each other.In quantum field theory, this separation of scales is typically valid, and the UV physics can be renormalized to obtain well-behaved predictions for IR physics. However, in certain generalizations of quantum field theory, such as noncommutative field theory and quantum gravity, it is expected that the interrelations between UV and IR physics become more prominent.In theories like string theory, which is a candidate theory of quantum gravity, it is anticipated that UV/IR mixing plays a crucial role. This means that the UV and IR physics are not entirely separate but are connected and intertwined in a nontrivial manner.UV/IR mixing implies that phenomena at high energies and short distances can have direct consequences for the qualitative features and behavior of systems at low energies and long distances, and vice versa. This departure from the strict separation of scales challenges the conventional understanding of the renormalization group paradigm in the context of these more generalized theories.In some cases, the explicit demonstration of UV/IR mixing has been achieved in specific models and frameworks. These demonstrations provide concrete evidence of the interrelations between UV and IR physics and highlight the need for a more integrated understanding of physical phenomena across different scales.The emergence of UV/IR mixing in theories beyond quantum field theory suggests a deeper connection between fundamental physics at various scales. Exploring and understanding these interrelations is essential for advancing our knowledge of quantum gravity, the nature of spacetime, and the fundamental structure of the universe.The study of UV/IR mixing opens up new avenues for research and theoretical exploration, pushing the boundaries of our understanding and challenging established notions. It offers insights into the interconnectedness of physical phenomena and provides opportunities to unravel the mysteries that lie at the interface of UV and IR physics.Overall, UV/IR mixing represents a departure from the strict separation of scales and highlights the intricate interplay between different energy scales in more generalized theories of physics. It emphasizes the need for a more comprehensive understanding of the interconnections between UV and IR physics and points towards new frontiers in theoretical exploration.1 / 1Noncommutative Perturbative Dynamics
Shiraz Minwalla, Mark Van Raamsdonk

Department of Physics, Princeton University

Abstract
We study the perturbative dynamics of noncommutative field theories on
R
d
, and find an
intriguing mixing of the UV and the IR. High energies of virtual particles in loops produce
non-analyticity at low momentum. Consequently, the low energy effective action is singular
at zero momentum even when the original noncommutative field theory is massive. Some of
the nonplanar diagrams of these theories are divergent, but we interpret these divergences
as IR divergences and deal with them accordingly. We explain how this UV/IR mixing
arises from the underlying noncommutativity. This phenomenon is reminiscent of the
channel duality of the double twist diagram in open string theory.

The authors find a fascinating interplay between the ultraviolet (UV) and infrared (IR) physics in these theories.One of the key findings is that high energies of virtual particles in loop diagrams lead to non-analytic behavior at low momenta. This nontrivial mixture between UV and IR phenomena is perhaps the most surprising
result of this paper. It is tempting to speculate that such mixing of scales could be relevant

In section 4 we point out that the IR singularities of section 3 are quite similar to the

surprising that a field theory exhibits such a stringy phenomenon.
In section 5 we explore higher order Feynman diagrams and some of their properties.
We also consider the limit of maximal noncommutativity, Î â â. In this limit the theory
is dominated by planar graphs and appears to be stringy.
In section 6 we explain how the noncommutativity of spacetime leads to the surprising
IR phenomena discussed in earlier sections. Section 7 is the beginning of an investigation
into the properties of noncommutative gauge theories. In an appendix we present the
expression for the Feynman integral corresponding to an arbitrary graph in a noncommutative theory, written in terms of Schwinger parameters.

1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe concept you described, where the largest scale of the observable universe is considered as an ultraviolet (UV) cutoff and the corresponding infrared (IR) cutoff is associated with the energy value of dark energy, is an intriguing idea that has been explored by Cohen and collaborators. 1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIndeed, as we move to larger size scales and lower energies, the effects of quantum mechanics can become less noticeable and appear to be "hidden" or obscured. This phenomenon is known as decoherence. Decoherence occurs when quantum systems interact with their surrounding environment, causing the loss of coherence and entanglement between particles or systems. As a result, the quantum properties of the system become blurred or "washed out," leading to the emergence of classical-like behavior.Decoherence plays a crucial role in our everyday experiences and is responsible for the absence of quantum weirdness at macroscopic scales. While quantum mechanics governs the behavior of particles at the microscopic level, the interactions of these particles with their environment quickly lead to the suppression of quantum effects. The large number of interactions and the complexity of the environment cause the delicate quantum superpositions and entanglement to break down, leading to classical-like behavior that we observe in our macroscopic world.From the viewpoint of a local observer, who is typically entangled with the surrounding environment, the effects of decoherence make quantum phenomena appear probabilistic and indeterminate, resembling classical behavior. This is known as the quantum-to-classical transition. It explains why we don't observe quantum superpositions or entanglement in our daily lives and why macroscopic objects appear to follow classical laws of physics.However, it's important to note that while decoherence accounts for the absence of quantum weirdness in macroscopic systems, quantum effects still play a crucial role at smaller scales and higher energies. In specialized laboratory conditions or isolated systems, where decoherence can be minimized, quantum phenomena such as superposition and entanglement can be observed and manipulated.Understanding the interplay between quantum mechanics and decoherence is an active area of research, as it has implications for fields such as quantum computing, quantum information theory, and the foundations of quantum physics. By studying the processes of decoherence and finding ways to control and mitigate its effects, scientists aim to develop new technologies and gain deeper insights into the fundamental nature of quantum mechanics.1 / 1While entanglement has been linked to UV/IR mixing in some research, the constraints identified by Cohen and his colleagues were primarily due to gravity rather than entanglement. However, recent work in string theory proposes that gravity itself may be disguised entanglement.
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe connection between entanglement and UV/IR mixing is an intriguing area of research in theoretical physics. While the constraints identified by Cohen and his colleagues primarily involve gravity, recent developments in string theory suggest a deep connection between gravity and entanglement.In string theory, which is a framework that aims to describe gravity and other fundamental forces in a consistent manner, the fundamental building blocks are not point-like particles but rather extended objects known as strings. Strings can vibrate in different modes, giving rise to various particles and fields in the theory.One remarkable feature of string theory is its holographic principle, which suggests that a higher-dimensional theory of gravity can be fully described by a lower-dimensional theory without gravity. This correspondence, known as the AdS/CFT (Anti-de Sitter/Conformal Field Theory) correspondence, relates a gravitational theory in a space with extra dimensions to a non-gravitational quantum field theory living on its boundary.Within this holographic framework, recent research has proposed that gravity itself may emerge from entanglement. According to this proposal, the geometry of spacetime, including gravity, could be a manifestation of entanglement patterns in a lower-dimensional quantum system. In other words, gravity could be viewed as a "disguised" form of entanglement.This idea, often referred to as the "entanglement gravity" or "entanglement-based approach to gravity," suggests a deep connection between entanglement and the emergence of spacetime and gravity. It provides a new perspective on how gravity might arise from more fundamental principles and hints at a possible unification of gravity and quantum mechanics.While the precise details and implications of the entanglement-based approach to gravity are still under active investigation, it represents an exciting direction for understanding the nature of gravity and its relationship to quantum phenomena. Exploring these ideas further could lead to profound insights into the fundamental nature of spacetime and the connections between entanglement, UV/IR mixing, and gravity.1 / 1This notion suggests a profound relationship between entanglement and UV/IR mixing. Well done!1 / 1Different notions of time and their partial relation to each other: Time is seen differently in various theories. For example, in Newtonian mechanics, it's absolute and universal, while in relativity, it's relative and can vary depending on the observer's motion and gravitational field.

The distinction between experiential time, irreversible time, and Newtonian time: Experiential time is the subjective experience of time, irreversible time is associated with entropy and the arrow of time, and Newtonian time is the classical, absolute concept of time.

Understanding time in general relativity and the absence of a preferred time variable: In general relativity, time is intertwined with space in a four-dimensional spacetime. Unlike Newtonian mechanics, there's no universally agreed-upon 'time variable'.

The arrow of time, entropy, and equilibration: The arrow of time refers to the one-way direction of time, often linked to the increase in entropy, which is related to the second law of thermodynamics.

Experiential time and its emotional underpinnings: The human perception of time can be influenced by emotions, psychology, and neurology.

Different intellectual perspectives on time and the need to separate intuitive experiential time from conceptual notions: Time as understood by physics can be very different from our everyday, intuitive understanding of time.

Viewing the universe as a unified quantum object linked by entanglement: Quantum entanglement, where particles become instantaneously connected regardless of distance, suggests a profound interconnectedness in the universe.

The use of effective field theories (EFTs) to describe phenomena at different scales: EFTs are a tool in theoretical physics to understand phenomena at a given energy scale without needing a detailed theory of higher energy processes.

The limitations and incompleteness of the standard model in particle physics: Despite its success, the Standard Model does not include gravity or account for dark matter and dark energy.

Exploring UV/IR mixing and its potential connections to dark energy and the Higgs problem: UV/IR mixing could provide insights into these outstanding puzzles in cosmology and particle physics.

The proposal that gravity itself may be disguised entanglement: This idea comes from attempts to reconcile quantum mechanics and general relativity, and suggests a deep connection between quantum entanglement and the structure of spacetime.

Actively seeking experimental evidence to support the concept of UV/IR mixing: Theoretical ideas need experimental verification. For UV/IR mixing, this could involve high energy physics experiments or astronomical observations.

the fundamental particles and their interactions, excluding gravity.Higgs boson: A particle associated with the Higgs field, which gives mass to other particles in the standard model.Dark energy: A (UV) and low-energy (IR) phenomena become interconnected.Entanglement: A quantum mechanical phenomenon where two or more particles become correlated in a way that their states are dependent on each other, regardless of the distance between them.Quantum gravity: A theoretical framework that attempts to reconcile the principles of quantum mechanics and general relativity to describe gravity at relativity: Einstein's theory of gravity that describes how matter and energy curve the fabric of spacetime.Arrow of time: The asymmetry of time, referring to the one-way direction of time from past to future.Spacetime: The four-dimensional framework that combines space and time in Einstein's theory of general relativity.Quantum mechanics: The branch of physics that describes the behavior of particles at microscopic scales, involving probabilistic outcomes and wave-particle duality.String theory: A theoretical framework that attempts to unify all fundamental forces by considering Time Notion	Properties
Experiential time	Subjective feeling of flowing speed. Emotionally charged.
Irreversible time	Oriented. Distinction between past and future.
Newtonian time	Unicity. Single "now" in the universe. Independent of relativistic phenomena.
Special relativistic time	Independent of actual events happening. Disregards relativistic gravity.
Cosmological time	Global perspective. Disregards inhomogeneity.
